  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1c50dfe3-42e4-4b94-a8e7-86996d1ecdc0'

  <span id="freeText9898578392289474582" style="display:none">First published in 1955, <i>A Night to Remember</i> remains a completely riveting account of the <i>Titanic</i>'s fatal collision and the behavior of the passengers and crew, both noble and ignominious. Some sacrificed their lives, while others fought like animals for their own survival. Wives beseeched husbands to join them in lifeboats; gentlemen went taut-lipped to their deaths in full evening dress; and hundreds of steerage passengers, trapped below decks, sought help in vain. <br /></span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Strengths: Interesting and quick read. Gives a pretty good account of the events that happened as the Titanic sunk particularly things that went wrong<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Strengths: Interesting and quick read. Gives a pretty good account of the events that happened as the Titanic sunk particularly things that went wrong leading to the massive loss of lives.<br /><br />Weaknesses: At some points it feels as if it provides to much extraneous detail (like what a bunch of different people were wearing at the time), at others it seems lacking in details. For example a bunch of names are thrown out but I never really felt like I "knew" who any of the people were.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1330819-the-theme-is-to-live-life-to-the-fullest">The theme is to live life to the fullest and with no regrets. A detail is that when the Titanic hit the iceberg people thought the big screeching noise was someone had dropped a plate in the kitchen. And once the people were finding out what actually happened. There was some that were thinking the &quot;what ifs&quot; What if I didn&#39;t get on this boat on this night would this still happen?</a>
</div>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/82241385-grace-naeve"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/82241385-grace-naeve">Grace Naeve</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I agree with this because I feel like once people figured out what actually happened they had no regrets. I feel like the people didn't regret getting<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I agree with this because I feel like once people figured out what actually happened they had no regrets. I feel like the people didn't regret getting on the boat because this was the boats first trip, and the boat was very nice and fancy. The people were living their lives to the fullest by taking this trip instead of staying home. I also think the Titanic crashing and sinking had nothing to do with the boat and how it was built, overall it was a nice boat. But the captain wanted to show off its speed and ended up crashing.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_89793334" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89793334" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1232712-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552603488p2/1232712.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/89793334?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/1232712-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1232712-matt?shelf=maritime-history">maritime-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1232712-matt?shelf=titanic">titanic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89793334" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5121249058909094100"><b>“All the [lifeboats] together could carry 1178 people. On this Sunday night there were 2207 on board the <i>Titanic</i>. This mathematical discrepancy was known by none of the passengers and few of the crew, but most of them wouldn’t have cared anyhow. The <i>Titanic</i> was unsinkable. Everybody said so. When Mrs. Albert Caldwell was watching the deck hands carry up luggage at Southampton, she asked one of them, ‘Is this ship really unsinkable?’ ‘Yes, lady,’ he answered. ‘God himself could not sink this ship</b></span>
  <span id="freeText5121249058909094100" style="display:none"><b>“All the [lifeboats] together could carry 1178 people. On this Sunday night there were 2207 on board the <i>Titanic</i>. This mathematical discrepancy was known by none of the passengers and few of the crew, but most of them wouldn’t have cared anyhow. The <i>Titanic</i> was unsinkable. Everybody said so. When Mrs. Albert Caldwell was watching the deck hands carry up luggage at Southampton, she asked one of them, ‘Is this ship really unsinkable?’ ‘Yes, lady,’ he answered. ‘God himself could not sink this ship.’”</b><br />-	Walter Lord, <i>A Night to Remember</i> <br /><br />James Cameron ruined the <i>Titanic</i>. <br /><br />Don’t get me wrong. It’s a near-great movie that – leaving aside the tired, mismatched love-story, and the atrocious dialogue – has one of the five-best set-piece action sequences in the long history of Hollywood. <br /><br />Still, the movie ruined <i>Titanic</i>, at least for those who already loved her. <br /><br />Sure, it was nice having the beautiful liner, with her sleek lines and awesome symmetry, once again dominating the world. The downside, though, was steep. Now, anyone who's ever been interested in the subject must contend with sideways glances from people who assume your curiosity was piqued by the teenage-catnip pairing of a young Kate Winslet and a young Leonardo DiCaprio “flying” on <i>Titanic</i>’s prow.<br /><br />Cameron’s <i>Titanic</i> ruled cinemas in 1997-98, breaking records and hoarding awards and filling the airwaves with Celine Dion. This came as a surprise to a lot of folks, but not those who had already been on the bandwagon, who recognized that the sinking of the <i>Titanic</i> is a near-perfect story of an incredibly imperfect voyage. <br /><br />Certainly, Walter Lord would not have been surprised. <br /><br />Back in 1955, when <i>A Night to Remember</i> was first published, <i>Titanic</i>’s fame had ebbed a bit. This tends to happen after a world war, a depression, and a second, bigger world war has killed, wounded, or dislocated tens of millions of people. Indeed, when Lord started corresponding with <i>Titanic</i> survivors, many of them expressed skepticism that anyone still cared. <br /><br />People did. People cared a great deal.<br /><br />Lord described himself in his own words as a writer of “living history.” He was an anecdotal historian who approached great big sweeping events through the perspectives of the individuals who lived them. Lord used the memories, experiences, and words of various eyewitnesses to tell his story. During his career, he effectively utilized this technique across a variety of subjects, including Pearl Harbor, the battle of Midway, and the siege of the Alamo, but never so effectively as in his certifiably-classic telling of the sinking of the R.M.S. <i>Titanic</i>. <br /><br />Lord's style is encompassed in the first two paragraphs:<br /><br /><blockquote>High in the crow's nest of the New White Star Liner <i>Titanic</i>, Lookout Frederick Fleet peered into a dazzling night. It was calm, clear and bitterly cold. There was no moon, but the cloudless sky blazed with stars. The Atlantic was like polished plate glass; people later said they had never seen it so smooth.<br /><br />This was the fifth night of the <i>Titanic</i>'s maiden voyage to New York, and it was already clear that she was not only the largest but also the most glamorous ship in the world. Even the passengers' dogs were glamorous. John Jacob Astor had along his Airedale Kitty. Henry Sleeper Harper, of the publishing family, had his prize Pekingese Sun Yat-sen...</blockquote><br /><br />Right away, you can see the amazing storytelling structure that Lord employs. He starts in the crow's nest, moments before the collision with the iceberg. He identifies one of his main characters, Fred Fleet, and then segues into a short riff on First Class pets. In a subsequent paragraph, Lord circles back to Fleet spotting the iceberg. Fleet warns the bridge and a tense 37 seconds elapse before the ship strikes the berg on its port side. At this point, Lord's tale starts to flower and expand. He leaves Fleet and the crow's nest to tell the stories of other people on different parts of the ship: a quartermaster on the aft docking bridge; a steward in First Class; a night baker baking rolls; passengers from all three classes. <br /><br />Lord doesn't employ a straight, linear narrative. Rather, <i>A Night to Remember</i> resembles a mosaic. An overarching picture of the tragedy is created out of dozens of individual accounts. Lord's genius is in weaving all these strands into a cohesive whole. He has a keen eye for dramatic moments and telling quotes. When he describes the ship's break-up, he does so by listing and contrasting all the different items breaking loose and crashing together, from the 29 boilers to a jeweled copy of the <i>Rubaiyat</i>, from 30,000 eggs to “a little mantel clock in B-38.” <br /><br />Lord is also a strong writer, which allows him to maintain the integrity of the personal observations of the survivors, while still delivering an exciting narrative. (It should be noted that Lord interviewed 63 survivors for <i>A Night to Remember</i>, and his letters with these men and women have become an important source for later <i>Titanic</i> historians). <br /><br /><blockquote>Down, down dipped the <i>Titanic</i>'s bow, and her stern swung slowly up. She seemed to be moving forward too. It was this motion which generated the wave that hit Daly, Brown, and dozens of others as it rolled aft...Lightoller watched the wave from the roof of the officer's quarters. He saw the crowds retreating up the deck ahead of it. He saw the nimbler ones keep clear, the slower ones overtaken and engulfed. He knew this kind of retreat just prolonged the agony. He turned and, facing the bow, dived in...</blockquote><br /><br /><i>A Night to Remember</i> is novelistic in its presentation, eschewing analysis and debate. For instance, rather than engage in a discussion about the band's final song, Lord simply chooses the Episcopal hymn <i>Autumn</i>, instead of <i>Nearer My God To Thee</i>. If you desire to know why Lord made that choice, you can read his follow up <i>The Night Lives On</i>, which is an in-depth treatment of a number of fascinating (if ultimately meaningless) questions (including First Officer William Murdoch's alleged suicide, an event blithely passed off as gospel in Cameron's <i>Titanic</i>, much to the chagrin of Murdoch's surviving relatives). <br /><br />I was five years old when <i>Titanic</i> was discovered, and probably ten when I read this book for the first time. Back then, the story of <i>Titanic</i> had real magic. Yes, it is human tragedy first and foremost; but it is also tragedy in the dramatic sense: the noblesse oblige of “women and children first;” Guggenheim dressing in his best to “die as a gentleman;” Ida Strauss refusing to leave her husband, who was not allowed in a lifeboat; the death of a titan set to music, and rockets, and finally the screams of fifteen-hundred people dropped into a freezing sea. <br /><br />Today, the only time <i>Titanic</i> is mentioned is when some new book or documentary tries to use cutting edge science to highlight some trivial new piece of evidence that is then blown out of all proportion. That is to be expected, I suppose. Even as the <i>Titanic</i>’s hulk rusts away on the Atlantic seabed, there are those looking to squeeze a few more bucks from her memory. Still, the endless slicing and dicing, the extreme forensic examinations, the listing of minutiae, cause me to forget why I gravitated towards the <i>Titanic</i> in the first place. <br /><br />Lord tells the <i>Titanic</i> story the way I hope it happened, and the way that the survivors remembered it. Knowing what we do about witness perception, and the tendency to embellish, Lord might have been a bit more critical of his interviewees. I mean, did Guggenheim really take the time to change into his dinner jacket before drowning? Did Captain Smith really step off the plunging bow and swim off into the night? No one can say for certain, yet some of these stories just sound too good to be true. They sound like bad fiction, rather than good history <br /><br />On the other hand, a lot of the witnesses turned out to be pretty darn perceptive. The great mystery that Ballard solved in 1985 was that <i>Titanic</i> had broken in two. Of course, young Jack Thayer had already said that, seventy-three years earlier, because it had happened fifty-yards from his seventeen year-old eyes. <br /><br />While the story of the <i>Titanic</i> has moved on, it has not entirely left <i>A Night to Remember</i> behind. It is, despite its minor flaws, still the best single book on the <i>Titanic</i>. Based on Lord's closeness to the actual participants – as well as his enormous talent – it will likely always retain that position.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5121249058909094100" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1662872264" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1662872264" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1662872264?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1662872264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13287698206024143587">Wow, I can see why this book is considered a classic in narrative nonfiction. In fact, I picked up this book because Nathaniel Philbrick, himself a master writer, told the New York Times that this was one of his favorite books of the genre. (The other nonfiction book he mentioned was Alfred Lansing's <i>Endurance</i>, which I also agree was excellent.)<br/><br/><i>A Night to Remember</i> gives a gripping, detailed account of what happened the night the Titanic hit an iceberg and sank in the Atlantic Ocean, killing more</span>
  <span id="freeText13287698206024143587" style="display:none">Wow, I can see why this book is considered a classic in narrative nonfiction. In fact, I picked up this book because Nathaniel Philbrick, himself a master writer, told the New York Times that this was one of his favorite books of the genre. (The other nonfiction book he mentioned was Alfred Lansing's <i>Endurance</i>, which I also agree was excellent.)<br /><br /><i>A Night to Remember</i> gives a gripping, detailed account of what happened the night the Titanic hit an iceberg and sank in the Atlantic Ocean, killing more than 1,500 people. Originally published in 1955, Walter Lord had interviewed survivors and reviewed documents to create this incredible narrative of the events surrounding April 15, 1912. I also liked the context Lord gave to the tragedy:<br /><br /><blockquote><br />Overriding everything else, the Titanic also marked the end of a general feeling of confidence. Until then men felt they had found the answer to a steady, orderly, civilized life. For 100 years the Western world had been at peace.* For 100 years technology had steadily improved. For 100 years the benefits of peace and industry seemed to be filtering satisfactorily through society. In retrospect, there may seem less grounds for confidence, but at the time most articulate people felt life was all right.<br /><br />The Titanic woke them up. Never again would they be quite so sure of themselves. In technology especially, the disaster was a terrible blow. Here was the "unsinkable ship" — perhaps man's greatest engineering achievement — going down the first time it sailed.<br /><br />But it went beyond that. If this supreme achievement was so terribly fragile, what about everything else? If wealth meant so little on this cold April night, did it mean so much the rest of the year? Scores of ministers preached that the Titanic was a heaven-sent lesson to awaken people from their complacency, to punish them for a top-heavy faith in material progress. If it was a lesson, it worked — people have never been sure of anything since.<br /></blockquote><br /><br /><i>*I think Mr. Lord has overlooked a few dozen wars in this eloquent-and-yet-untrue sentence, including the American Civil War, the Napoleonic wars, and innumerable conflicts involving the British Empire. Other than that, this passage is great.</i><br /><br />I listened to this book on audio and was so engrossed I finished it in one session. Highly recommended.<br /><br /><b>Favorite Quote</b><br />"What troubled people especially was not just the tragedy — or even its needlessness — but the element of fate in it all. If the Titanic had heeded any of the six ice messages on Sunday ... if ice conditions had been normal ... if the night had been rough or moonlit ... if she had seen the berg 15 seconds sooner — or 15 seconds later ... if she had hit the ice any other way ... if her watertight bulkheads had been one deck higher ... if she had carried enough boats ... if the Californian had only come ... Had any one of those 'ifs' turned out right, every life might have been saved. But they all went against her — a classic Greek tragedy."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13287698206024143587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_104603752" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/104603752" />
    <a title="Lobstergirl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl"><img alt="Lobstergirl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380256801p2/1910836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/104603752?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lobstergirl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lobstergirl" href="/user/show/1910836-lobstergirl">Lobstergirl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer104603752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1230888588065935981">James Cameron's vision of the Titanic decided that the most compelling and lucrative story would focus on two young lovers who had just met. Looking at the passenger manifest, where survivors are listed in italics and the dead are not, suggests how blandly offensive this vision is. It's hard to argue with the chivalry of "women and children first," but for family after family, particularly among first class passengers, fathers and husbands went down with the ship while mothers, wives, and kiddie</span>
  <span id="freeText1230888588065935981" style="display:none">James Cameron's vision of the Titanic decided that the most compelling and lucrative story would focus on two young lovers who had just met. Looking at the passenger manifest, where survivors are listed in italics and the dead are not, suggests how blandly offensive this vision is. It's hard to argue with the chivalry of "women and children first," but for family after family, particularly among first class passengers, fathers and husbands went down with the ship while mothers, wives, and kiddies (and often the female servants of the very wealthy) rowed away in lifeboats. Arthur Ryerson, scion of the steel and iron family, took off his lifebelt when he saw that his wife's maid, Victorine, didn't have one. Ryerson, his wife, and three of their children were returning from France to the U.S. for the funeral of his son, who had been thrown from a car the week before. Ryerson Senior perished. John Jacob Astor asked if he could accompany his wife, who was pregnant, into a boat; request denied. She and her maid survived; Astor and his manservant died. A strange calm descended over the doomed elite: Benjamin Guggenheim and his valet changed into their evening clothes so they could "go down like gentlemen." Mrs. Isador Straus refused to leave her husband (the founder of Macy's) and they watched the hubbub, arms entwined, as in another part of the ship steerage passengers, many of whom didn't speak English, clutched rosaries and prayed. But character was not uniformly spread amongst the nobility. As the ship disappeared beneath the waves, Lady Cosmo Duff Gordon in Lifeboat 1 remarked to her secretary: "There is your beautiful nightdress gone."<br /><br />Lord engagingly writes of these swells:<br /><br /><blockquote>There was a wonderful intimacy about this little world of the Edwardian rich. There was no flicker of surprise when they bumped into each other, whether at the Pyramids (a great favorite), the Cowes regatta, or the springs at Baden-Baden. They seemed to get the same ideas at the same time, and one of these ideas was to make the maiden voyage of the largest ship in the world.</blockquote><br /><br />The sinking of the Titanic marked the end of an era in many ways, Lord argues, fairly convincingly. The American aristocracy ceased being noble and became merely wealthy. The sense of <i>noblesse oblige</i> went. People continued to make fortunes, but the war and the income tax bit into the unrelieved joyousness of being obscenely moneyed. "Men would go on being brave, but never again would they be brave in quite the same way." </span>
  <a data-text-id="1230888588065935981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3525926178" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3525926178" />
    <a title="Juli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38617426-juli"><img alt="Juli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453642808p2/38617426.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3525926178?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juli" href="/user/show/38617426-juli">Juli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781441743916" href="/book/show/8358982-a-night-to-remember">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38617426-juli?shelf=read-audiobooks-2020">read-audiobooks-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3525926178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4827740672108872064">Today's cruise ships are basically floating cities. Able to carry more than 6000 people, the Oasis of the Seas (Royal Caribbean Line) is 5 times the size of the Titanic. But back in its day, more than 100 years ago, the Titanic was a wonder. It took thousands of men more than 2 years to build her. Titanic was 4 city blocks long and could carry more than 2,400 people. She was new....she was massive....and she was doomed. 2 years to build.....and the largest ship afloat in April 1912 took just und</span>
  <span id="freeText4827740672108872064" style="display:none">Today's cruise ships are basically floating cities. Able to carry more than 6000 people, the Oasis of the Seas (Royal Caribbean Line) is 5 times the size of the Titanic. But back in its day, more than 100 years ago, the Titanic was a wonder. It took thousands of men more than 2 years to build her. Titanic was 4 city blocks long and could carry more than 2,400 people. She was new....she was massive....and she was doomed. 2 years to build.....and the largest ship afloat in April 1912 took just under 3 hours to sink. <br /><br />Walter Lord tells the story of one of the most famous ocean disasters from before the ship struck an iceberg to the aftermath of the sinking. Walter Lord interviewed more than 60 survivors of the disaster to write the book. A Night to Remember was an instant bestseller in 1955. A film version was released in 1958. Lord even consulted on the filming of the 1997 movie Titanic. <br /><br /> I'm not sure why the fate of the Titanic is such a compelling story. It might be the huge loss of lives, the loss of such a grand ship on its first voyage, passengers with such disparate lives all doomed to the same fate....or a combination of all of it. First Class passenger John Jacob Astor, one of the wealthiest men in the world in 1912, drowned in the Atlantic alongside poor immigrants from steerage. It hits home when you compare ticket prices in today's money....those who paid the equivalent of $50,000 for a first class passage died alongside those who struggled to raise the $460 for a steerage ticket. Lord hits home with the difference in treatment of the classes on board when he points out that only one first class child died....but 52 children from steerage perished. Some passengers in steerage never even made it up to the boat deck for a chance of a seat in a lifeboat. <br /><br />I'm sure it's his interviews with so many survivors that makes this book so realistic. His descriptions are vivid and made me feel like I was almost there. I listened to the audio version of this book. A combination of Lord's story-telling and Fred Williams excellent narration kept me engrossed in the story from start to finish. I have read many many books on the Titanic, watched movies, listened to podcasts....for me, it's a story I just seem obsessed with. It's horrific...and mesmerizing at the same time. Lord makes the story about the people....not just the event. He tells the story of an Italian woman crying for her children on board the Carpathia, only to be reunited with them both; the first class passenger who refused to leave her Great Dane on board the ship so perished with her dog; and the stunned silence of the women in the lifeboats as they realized they had just witnessed more than 1000 people drown. It's about more than a luxurious boat that didn't survive its first Atlantic crossing.....it's about the loss of more than 1,500 people and the story of the last 3 hours of their lives. <br /><br />Great book! The audio (Blackstone) is just shy of 5 1/2 hours long. Fred Williams does a great job of narrating. He reads at a steady pace and has a nice voice. Very entertaining listening experience. <br /><br />Walter Lord also wrote books about Dunkirk and the attack on Pearl Harbor. I've got both on my TBR list now!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4827740672108872064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_58047694" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58047694" />
    <a title="Evan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1248986-evan"><img alt="Evan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488574589p2/1248986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58047694?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan" href="/user/show/1248986-evan">Evan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553278279" href="/book/show/228301.A_Night_to_Remember">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=survival">survival</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=arr-matey-seagoing-fare">arr-matey-seagoing-fare</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=2009-reads">2009-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=adventure-truelife">adventure-truelife</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=freezin-ur-ass-off">freezin-ur-ass-off</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=__in-my-collection">__in-my-collection</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58047694" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9774894981442404777">This is sort of the primary, classic book on the Titanic disaster. Published in 1955, it's short and smoothly written -- covering the viewpoints of a large cast and changing centers of perspective with ease. There have been four movies made about the Titanic in the sound era (there were several silent movies about or loosely based on it). I've seen three of the four and have the other one on VHS to watch. The first was a 1943 German, Nazi-produced spectacle that mainly was made, it seemed, as an</span>
  <span id="freeText9774894981442404777" style="display:none">This is sort of the primary, classic book on the Titanic disaster. Published in 1955, it's short and smoothly written -- covering the viewpoints of a large cast and changing centers of perspective with ease. There have been four movies made about the Titanic in the sound era (there were several silent movies about or loosely based on it). I've seen three of the four and have the other one on VHS to watch. The first was a 1943 German, Nazi-produced spectacle that mainly was made, it seemed, as an anti-British propaganda piece. The special effects were so good that the ship sinking model shots were re-used in the 1958 Brit version, based on this book: "A Night to Remember." In the interim, Hollywood made an attempt in 1953, called simply "Titanic," starring Barbara Stanwyck. For some reason, I've never found the time to watch it, even though I own it (*see 2016 addendum, bottom of review). I find it hard to imagine that it could surpass the 1958 British film: a soberly compelling version that remains my favorite. It seems most in spirit of the book.<br />James Cameron's 1997 version is for little girls. Blah.<br /><br />Reading on...rating soon to come.<br /><br />This is a breeze to read. Very vivid, full of detail. The only thing that causes a slight slowdown is the sheer number of characters. To Lord's credit, he reminds us frequently of the positions and titles of the characters, so we don't have to go back or jog our memories trying to remember who these people are. I love when authors do that. I'm for easy. As I'm reading this I'm realizing how well the 1958 film captured this account and how badly the hokey 1997 film did.<br /><br />FINAL:<br />Enjoyed this greatly. I especially enjoyed Lord's analysis of the class snobbery and attitudes of the time that led to a higher percentage of deaths among the third-class passengers vs. the first and second classes, and the media's disinterest at the time to hearing the stories of the common people in preference to the Astors and the other robber-baron types. On the other hand, he is fair, and gives credit to almost everyone for having class and dignity. I hesitate to call Lord's treatment of the issues "socially conscious," I just think he was trying to be more "fair and balanced" as a historian than other writers had been previously.<br />There are probably other books that go into greater detail on certain aspects of this story, but I can't imagine there being a better entire book on the Titanic than this.<br /><br />--------<br />* Addendum: 2016:<br /><br />In the intervening years since I wrote this review, I did end up seeing the 1953 Titanic movie, and it is an entertaining potboiler vehicle for Barbara Stanwyck, all gussied up in high-gloss duds and 20th-Century Fox production values and familial bad blood. Kind of <i>Stella Dallas</i> on the high seas. Barbara can suffer in mink just as well on a cruise liner as in a mansion. It's grand entertainment, but not a very good Titanic movie. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9774894981442404777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12274271406615506254">Book on CD read by Walter Jarvis<br/><br/>On April 15, 1912, the greatest ship to ever sail struck an iceberg and sank in the North Atlantic. This is a chronological tale of what the people aboard the <i>Titanic</i> recall of that night’s events. <br/><br/>This is a re-read. I first read it before I joined either Shelfari or Goodreads, so I have no record of when I read it. I believe it was in the 1980s; I know it was long before the hugely successful movie starring Leonardo DiCaprio and Kate Winslet. If memory serves, I </span>
  <span id="freeText12274271406615506254" style="display:none">Book on CD read by Walter Jarvis<br /><br />On April 15, 1912, the greatest ship to ever sail struck an iceberg and sank in the North Atlantic. This is a chronological tale of what the people aboard the <i>Titanic</i> recall of that night’s events. <br /><br />This is a re-read. I first read it before I joined either Shelfari or Goodreads, so I have no record of when I read it. I believe it was in the 1980s; I know it was long before the hugely successful movie starring Leonardo DiCaprio and Kate Winslet. If memory serves, I re-read it at about the time the movie was released. So this is my third reading.<br /><br />It’s a gripping story, and Lord does a great job of bringing all these people to life. I get a real sense of the confusion and disbelief when the ship first strikes the iceberg. And later, of the chaos and panic when it is clear she will go down, and there are not enough lifeboats for everyone aboard to safely get away. <br /><br />Lord used transcripts of testimony given by many people during the inquiry following the disaster, as well as personal interviews with survivors and relatives of those lost at sea, as well as people who were aboard the <i>Carpathia</i> which picked up all the lifeboats and returned with them to New York. The text edition I had included some photographs, as well as a full list of the passengers.<br /><br />Walter Jarvis does an okay job of reading the audio version, but I really disliked his voice. Still, he did convey a sense of urgency as he related the events of that horrible night. <br /></span>

<div id="review_583748835" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/583748835" />
    <a title="Bill Rogers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18019148-bill-rogers"><img alt="Bill Rogers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365346770p2/18019148.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/583748835?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Rogers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Rogers" href="/user/show/18019148-bill-rogers">Bill Rogers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13600124-a-night-to-remember">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer583748835" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer946243711759813755">Because I'm cruel and evil, I'm going to ruin this book for you with a spoiler. The ship sinks, folks.<br/><br/>What, you already knew that? You've heard the story before, once or twice, maybe? In fact, do you think the Titanic story is overblown in our culture? Are you tired of it? You can blame Walter Lord. But don't blame him too much; he wrote an amazing book.<br/><br/>Lord was something of a harmless crank with a bit of a fascination with this big honkin' ship that had run into an iceberg a few decades before.</span>
  <span id="freeText946243711759813755" style="display:none">Because I'm cruel and evil, I'm going to ruin this book for you with a spoiler. The ship sinks, folks.<br /><br />What, you already knew that? You've heard the story before, once or twice, maybe? In fact, do you think the Titanic story is overblown in our culture? Are you tired of it? You can blame Walter Lord. But don't blame him too much; he wrote an amazing book.<br /><br />Lord was something of a harmless crank with a bit of a fascination with this big honkin' ship that had run into an iceberg a few decades before. He collected all the information on it he could. This being the 1950s, he then topped that off by interviewing many of the survivors of that disaster. (The fact that this was not that long after the Titanic sank, in terms of history, is pointed out by the fact that one of the Titanic stewards Lord interviewed was still working on trans-Atlantic passenger liners at the time the book came out.)<br /><br />Lord then wrote his book, for the most part, as anecdotes from people who were there, assembled like jigsaw pieces into a coherent picture. It is a brilliant and compelling way of telling the story because it gives you the overall picture, the names and faces of the people who stood on the slanting deck that cold night, some unlikely and near-forgotten heroes and villains, and the sense that you're right there watching it happen.<br /><br />A Night to Remember is a quick and easy read, and very rewarding. I recommend it. In fact, if you want to know about the Titanic disaster, I suggest you read this book, watch the movie of the same name that was made from it, and skip the eternal, tedious, and repetitive rest of the literature on the subject.</span>
  <a data-text-id="946243711759813755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_312270865" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/312270865" />
    <a title="Kat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3835702-kat"><img alt="Kat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357322828p2/3835702.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/312270865?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat" href="/user/show/3835702-kat">Kat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13600124-a-night-to-remember">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3835702-kat?shelf=udate">udate</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3835702-kat?shelf=non-fict">non-fict</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer312270865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer499197768527130580">When I was about 15, I was completely obsessed with the Titanic (yep, that's the year the movie came out!), and I brought every book I could find about it. And at the time, hyping up the movie, there was a lot of books available.<br/><br/>A couple of years later, the obsession had faded and it wasn't until the 100th anniversary of the sinking in mid-April that my interest was piqued again. So I picked up a copy of A Night to Remember.<br/><br/>Written in 1955, it reads with a surprisingly modern and appealing voice</span>
  <span id="freeText499197768527130580" style="display:none">When I was about 15, I was completely obsessed with the Titanic (yep, that's the year the movie came out!), and I brought every book I could find about it. And at the time, hyping up the movie, there was a lot of books available.<br /><br />A couple of years later, the obsession had faded and it wasn't until the 100th anniversary of the sinking in mid-April that my interest was piqued again. So I picked up a copy of A Night to Remember.<br /><br />Written in 1955, it reads with a surprisingly modern and appealing voice - it's not stuffy or wordy in it's explanations of what happened that fateful night, and although the 'cast of characters' is long, it's an extremely riveting read.<br /><br />Using interviews with passengers from first, second and third class and crew as a basis for the book, Walter Lord's classic has stood the test of time well. Although the cast of characters is large and complicated, the more prominent passengers (Mrs. Brown, John Jacob Astor and Benjamin Guggenheim) stand out, as do the chilling accounts from below-decks crew and steerage passengers. There are stories of miraculous survival and heart-breaking stories of final goodbyes, and coverage of the rescue and the landing of the survivors in New York.<br /><br />As a non-fiction book, this is not a dry read at all. Sure, it's got a whole lot of facts about the ship, the sinking and the rescue efforts, but it's presented in an easy-to-read language, interspersed with amazing true stories.<br /><br />Read more of my reviews at <a href="http://theaussiezombie.blogspot.com" rel="nofollow noopener">The Aussie Zombie</a></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_911198918" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/911198918" />
    <a title="Shaun" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9779623-shaun"><img alt="Shaun" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565572877p2/9779623.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/911198918?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shaun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shaun" href="/user/show/9779623-shaun">Shaun</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9779623-shaun?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9779623-shaun?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer911198918" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12868696288926880860"><b>If...</b><br/><br/><blockquote><b>If</b> the <i>Titanic</i> had heeded any of the six ice messages on Sunday....<b>if</b> ice conditions had been normal...<b>if</b> the night had been rough or moonlit...<b>if</b> she had seen the berg 15 seconds sooner--or later...<b>if</b> she had hit the ice any other way...<b>if</b> her watertight bulkheads had been one deck higher...<b>if</b> she had carried enough boats...<b>if</b> the <i>Californian</i> had only come. Had any one of these "ifs" turned out right, every life might have been saved. But they all went against her--a classic Greek tragedy.</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText12868696288926880860" style="display:none"><b>If...</b><br /><br /><blockquote><b>If</b> the <i>Titanic</i> had heeded any of the six ice messages on Sunday....<b>if</b> ice conditions had been normal...<b>if</b> the night had been rough or moonlit...<b>if</b> she had seen the berg 15 seconds sooner--or later...<b>if</b> she had hit the ice any other way...<b>if</b> her watertight bulkheads had been one deck higher...<b>if</b> she had carried enough boats...<b>if</b> the <i>Californian</i> had only come. Had any one of these "ifs" turned out right, every life might have been saved. But they all went against her--a classic Greek tragedy.</blockquote><br /><br />This was a fairly concise recounting--perhaps the definitive recounting--of the tragedy known as the Titanic. I might have found this more compelling if I hadn't already watched a number of documentaries on the topic. There was really very little new here for me. I did learn that in 1898 a struggling author named Morgan Robertson had written a novel about a fictional luxury liner, which he named the <i>Titan</i>, that was eerily similar to the <i>Titanic</i> (so much so that the description of his imaginary ship perfectly described the <i>Titanic</i>). Most disturbing, however, is that Robertson's ship, which coincidentally was also labeled as "unsinkable," also struck an iceberg and sank on a cold April night.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12868696288926880860" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1813164776840856482">I've never trusted the month of April. It should be the month of flowers and bunnies and eggs and bees, which it is. But April is also the month of disasters...the 1906 Earthquake and Fire, Chernobyl, the Oklahoma City Bombing, Columbine and, of course, the Sinking of the Titanic. The 'S' is capitalized.<br/><br/>Prior to reading Walter Lord's version of the Sinking, the Titanic was just another shipwreck to me, but forever after, it is THE shipwreck. Under Lord's framing, it's also the end of the Gilded </span>
  <span id="freeText1813164776840856482" style="display:none">I've never trusted the month of April. It should be the month of flowers and bunnies and eggs and bees, which it is. But April is also the month of disasters...the 1906 Earthquake and Fire, Chernobyl, the Oklahoma City Bombing, Columbine and, of course, the Sinking of the Titanic. The 'S' is capitalized.<br /><br />Prior to reading Walter Lord's version of the Sinking, the Titanic was just another shipwreck to me, but forever after, it is THE shipwreck. Under Lord's framing, it's also the end of the Gilded Age when industrial magnates could wear warm furs while sitting top deck on the greatest ocean liner ever built. The stories of the survivors and the drowned so shook me, I remember having to carry a flashlight to bed so I could read this adventure under covers without anyone else in the house knowing. There are a number of books about the Titanic, but there really is only one. Lord's Titanic.<br /><br /><i>I will show you fear in a handful of dust</i><br /><br />Yes, April is the cruellest month.<br /><br />Book Season = Spring (still won't travel by boat)</span>

<div id="review_463999769" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

He also draws some interesting cultural conclusions which point to its place in history and why it still fascinates us.</span>

<div id="review_2097446712" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2097446712" />
    <a title="Manybooks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks"><img alt="Manybooks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459773375p2/2970379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2097446712?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manybooks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manybooks" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks">Manybooks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=titanic">titanic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=disasters">disasters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=book-reviews">book-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2097446712" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12677998366208817033">Walter Lord's <u>A Night to Remember</u> (which I absolutely adored as a teenager) is basically and for all intents and purposes a live action, riveting account of the sinking of the Titanic, from start to finish, from the time the iceberg was hit to when the sadly oh so very few survivors were picked up, had finally reached the Carpathia (and I can well understand how and why this novel was made into a movie, although I have not seen it). <br/><br/>Now as a teenager, the massive amounts of emotionally fraught p</span>
  <span id="freeText12677998366208817033" style="display:none">Walter Lord's <u>A Night to Remember</u> (which I absolutely adored as a teenager) is basically and for all intents and purposes a live action, riveting account of the sinking of the Titanic, from start to finish, from the time the iceberg was hit to when the sadly oh so very few survivors were picked up, had finally reached the Carpathia (and I can well understand how and why this novel was made into a movie, although I have not seen it). <br /><br />Now as a teenager, the massive amounts of emotionally fraught presented textual information and details were very much intriguing and certainly kept me hooked and reading all night (as I started <u>A Night to Remember</u> at around nine in the evening after I had completed my required homework and finished at six in the morning the next day) and I still even now much appreciate how both compassionately and with a sense of fair play, Walter Lord tells his story, recounts the sinking of the Titanic and the tragedy of the so many lives lost. However, during my recent reread, precisely those very aspects of <u>A Night to Remember</u> I so loved as a teenager, namely the informative often in ones' face textual and narrative details, the cinematic, play-by-play action and scope of events, I am finding at times and actually even generally more than a bit overused, overdone, exaggerated and simply too much, with the reader almost overcome by the latter and so much so that the main and necessary areas of criticism, the rather callous and lighthearted ignoring of the received warnings of icebergs because the Titanic was deemed unsinkable and especially that there were not enough lifeboats for the passengers and that steerage, that third class passengers were generally and even quite deliberately ignored, that due to their social standing, their lives were not deemed as valuable as those travelling in first or second class, almost swept away (and no pun intended) with and by the overabundance and meticulous descriptions of even the smallest and most insignificant points of information (in other words that for older adult I, <u>A Night to Remember</u> reads and feels as just too annoyingly cinematic). Still recommended and a historically accurate and often painfully emotional read, but while as a teenager, I would have definitely rated Walter Lord's <u>A Night to Remember</u> with four stars, I now can and will only consider a high two star ranking at best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12677998366208817033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3016333338" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3016333338" />
    <a title="Sean Chick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5936947-sean-chick"><img alt="Sean Chick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1326522888p2/5936947.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3016333338?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Chick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Chick" href="/user/show/5936947-sean-chick">Sean Chick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553278279" href="/book/show/228301.A_Night_to_Remember">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3016333338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8264842884997024613">The more I read about the Titanic the more I dislike James Cameron's film, with its reliance on spectacle, mockery/slander of historical figures (Lightoller, Captain Smith, Guggenheim, etc.), and a corny love story, when the real sinking was a horror show. Sadly, Cameron is not alone, as nearly every film (there are over 15 feature length movies) is based on fictional characters although the actual sinking contains a wealth of stories. Many of them are here in this account, done in a documentary</span>
  <span id="freeText8264842884997024613" style="display:none">The more I read about the Titanic the more I dislike James Cameron's film, with its reliance on spectacle, mockery/slander of historical figures (Lightoller, Captain Smith, Guggenheim, etc.), and a corny love story, when the real sinking was a horror show. Sadly, Cameron is not alone, as nearly every film (there are over 15 feature length movies) is based on fictional characters although the actual sinking contains a wealth of stories. Many of them are here in this account, done in a documentary style, blow by blow, as Titanic hits an Iceberg, sinks, and the survivors are brought to New York City. While the names can sometimes start to mesh together, the tight and sparse style has the effect of making the book seem utterly reliable and unsensational, so that the moments of horror, heroism, cowardice, and humor seem more real than other books that lapse into purple prose. <br /><br />The book is very much a window into the social concerns and blind spots of its day. While the book remains focused on individuals, class is important. Lord supposed that the world of 1912, when 3rd class was kept at bay from 1st and 2nd class was at an end. The arch of history though does not "bend towards justice" but instead toward decline, death, and change. The working class, at the height of its powers in 1955 (when this was published), is now thoroughly abused by the right, left, and center. Furthermore, the racism Lord comments on is one we have partially forgotten in the obsession with "whiteness." On Titanic it was the Anglo-Saxon and Nordic feeling of superiority to the Irish, French, Spanish, Polish, Italian, Greek, Chinese, and Japanese. We can argue that that world ended post World War II, but I doubt we are far enough removed from history or human nature as shown by the interplay in world politics not to mention personal experiences. <br /><br />Lastly, Lord does not despise the upper class of the day. He notes their shortcomings but cannot help but admire how the three richest men onboard (Astor, Strauss, Guggenheim) faced and accepted death. Could you see any of our corporate overlords, replete with rainbow flags and assurances of their morale superiority, doing the same? I am convinced most would either force their way onto to a boat, or fall crying to their knees. Guggenheim, by contrast, was last seen declaring "We are dressed in our best and are prepared to go down as gentlemen."</span>
  <a data-text-id="8264842884997024613" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8355802701220161265">I have tried many times to read A Night To Remember, and I always ended-up putting it aside. <br/><br/>This time, however, the reading experience was quite different. Six days earlier, I started 'Shadow of the Titanic: The Extraordinary Stories Of Those Who Survived', and as I learned a lot about survivors' post-Titanic life, I formed some kind of an attachment and an understanding to them; thus, I guess, I was meant to read A Night To Remember, the story of the sinking right after reading about the event</span>
  <span id="freeText8355802701220161265" style="display:none">I have tried many times to read A Night To Remember, and I always ended-up putting it aside. <br /><br />This time, however, the reading experience was quite different. Six days earlier, I started 'Shadow of the Titanic: The Extraordinary Stories Of Those Who Survived', and as I learned a lot about survivors' post-Titanic life, I formed some kind of an attachment and an understanding to them; thus, I guess, I was meant to read A Night To Remember, the story of the sinking right after reading about the events that took place after it. <br /><br />A Night To Remember was an immersive experience that I won't be able to forget. As I continued with each page, I kept picturing what was happening in my mind and was able to place myself behind these people's shoulders who witnessed the unimaginable. <br /><br />The book was concise, easy to read at times (but sometimes not), and it provided everything that I needed to know about the Titanic's definitive last hours.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_338874944" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/338874944" />
    <a title="Geevee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5153787-geevee"><img alt="Geevee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598890966p2/5153787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/338874944?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geevee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geevee" href="/user/show/5153787-geevee">Geevee</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141399690" href="/book/show/13568430-a-night-to-remember">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5153787-geevee?shelf=british-history">british-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5153787-geevee?shelf=american-history">american-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5153787-geevee?shelf=european-history">european-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5153787-geevee?shelf=transport">transport</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer338874944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer183793322515337980">Walter Lord's book is itself over half a century old (published 1956) and yet it remains highly readable justifying its "classic" tag.<br/><br/>Where Lord excels is that he interviewed 63 survivors and weaved their recollections with many other written sources and testimonies to tell a story. <br/><br/>And a fine story it is. He hooks the reader on the first page by placing you firmly in 1912 and on board the second of White Star Line's Olympic class ships, and at the time of her voyage the largest ship afloat, and</span>
  <span id="freeText183793322515337980" style="display:none">Walter Lord's book is itself over half a century old (published 1956) and yet it remains highly readable justifying its "classic" tag.<br /><br />Where Lord excels is that he interviewed 63 survivors and weaved their recollections with many other written sources and testimonies to tell a story. <br /><br />And a fine story it is. He hooks the reader on the first page by placing you firmly in 1912 and on board the second of White Star Line's Olympic class ships, and at the time of her voyage the largest ship afloat, and although one knows the ending, you are immediately thrust into the night's events and begin to meet some of the people wrapped up in a world changing night that will as the reader knows end in tragedy. <br /><br />Not only does Lord use the interviews with the survivors to create atmoshpere and context but, as importantly for the story and the reader's information, he also includes people who were onboard the Carpathia. So we read of events before the iceberg strikes the Titanic and the aftermath in the cold calm waters of the ice ridden sea through to the passengers on the Carpathia thinking their own ship is in trouble because blankets are being collected and lifeboats readied. <br /><br />The disaster of the Titanic changed the way ships and their passengers, communications and safety equipment were treated and arranged thereafter. A Night to Remember changed the way historic events were written about by using first person accounts and testimony. <br /><br />100 years on from the sinking of this most famous ship the people's story continues to be told by a classic genre changing piece of storytelling.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="183793322515337980" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2713062800" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Yousra Serry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7173246-yousra-serry"><img alt="Yousra Serry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563190290p2/7173246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2713062800?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yousra Serry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yousra Serry" href="/user/show/7173246-yousra-serry">Yousra Serry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780606011372" href="/book/show/7121239-night-to-remember">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2713062800" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11177018921447802366"><b>I watched this video showing the sinking of the Titanic in real time while reading this book to try and get the most authentic experience of this tragedy. <br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rs9w5bgtJC8'>https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rs9w5...</a><br/><br/>And.. <i>wow</i>.<br/>Absolutely amazing. <br/>A must read for anyone who was as obsessed with the Titanic as I was when I was a kid. <span id="freeTextContainer16232877718808919231">This has been on my TBR list for a while, but I felt an urge to get it read earlier this year, given that I was going on a cruise myself. I decided it would be best left until <i>after</i> I returned, just in case it made me a little edgy!!<br/><br/>It was an interesting read, although there wasn’t anything of major importance that I wasn’t already aware of - but still amazing to think that people were firmly convinced that the <i>Titanic</i> was unsinkable. Although, having cruised on a large ocean liner now myself, I</span>
  <span id="freeText16232877718808919231" style="display:none">This has been on my TBR list for a while, but I felt an urge to get it read earlier this year, given that I was going on a cruise myself. I decided it would be best left until <i>after</i> I returned, just in case it made me a little edgy!!<br /><br />It was an interesting read, although there wasn’t anything of major importance that I wasn’t already aware of - but still amazing to think that people were firmly convinced that the <i>Titanic</i> was unsinkable. Although, having cruised on a large ocean liner now myself, I feel like its sinking would also be very unlikely - it just feels so solid, however I guess that was how people felt about the <i>Titanic</i> as well!! <br /><br />Of course, there would be much more rigorous safety standards now than there were then, not to mention adequate lifeboats. If the sinking of the <i>Titanic</i> taught us nothing else, it is that there simply must be an adequate number of lifeboats for all passengers and crew aboard. I’m sure the procedures for mustering and filling the lifeboats would also be much more rigorous now than occurred on that evening.<br /><br />I liked this quote toward the end of the book, which seemed to underline the misfortune of the <i>Titanic</i> - “What troubled people especially was not just the tragedy - or even its needlessness - but the element of fate in it all. If the Titanic had heeded any of the six ice messages on Sunday…if ice conditions had been normal…if the night had been rough or moonlit…if she had seen the berg 15 seconds sooner - or 15 seconds later…if she had hit the ice any other way…if her watertight bulkheads had been one deck higher…if she had carried enough boats…if the <i>Californian</i> had only come. Had any one of these “ifs” turned out right, every life might have been saved. But they all went against her - a classic Greek tragedy."</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=memoirs">memoirs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>,

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16495645902324383168">They say that this book is the definitive source when it comes to the story of <i>Titanic</i> and I agree. I learned so much details from this book that I did not see in the James Cameron 1997 hit. Even if I watched that movie 20+ times (and still occasionally have that urge), I still had that insatiable need to know more about what happened. But when I finally closed this book? Enough, I said. I'm truly satiated.<br/><br/><i>A Night to Remember</i> is 1955 Walter Lord's (1917-2002) non-fiction work detailing what happ</span>
  <span id="freeText16495645902324383168" style="display:none">They say that this book is the definitive source when it comes to the story of <i>Titanic</i> and I agree. I learned so much details from this book that I did not see in the James Cameron 1997 hit. Even if I watched that movie 20+ times (and still occasionally have that urge), I still had that insatiable need to know more about what happened. But when I finally closed this book? Enough, I said. I'm truly satiated.<br /><br /><i>A Night to Remember</i> is 1955 Walter Lord's (1917-2002) non-fiction work detailing what happened in the RMS Titanic on April 12, 1912. During his writing he tracked down and interviewed 63 of the surviving passengers of the ship and he also became a consultant to James Cameron during the filming of the blockbuster disaster movie of all times.<br /><br />Due to these first-hand accounts of the 63 survivors, the book seems like you see the disaster by yourself through their vivid accounts. One thing that struck me (that I saw in the movie but did not make a mark in my mind) was how social class was accepted by the passengers even during the time of loading to the getaway boats. For example, when the last boat was brought down, the crew did not know that there were still women and children from the third level that are still waiting for the gates to open so they can get the priority. And those women and children accepted their fate because they are not the priorities compared to the 40+ men from the upper decks (levels) who got into the earlier boats. Some of these men disguised as women so they could aboard those boats.<br /><br />I really liked this book. It was worth every minute I spent pouring each page. I just can't stop my fascination about this ship that was declared to be unsinkable even by God!<br /><br />And yet it sank.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16495645902324383168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
